# Sequential Gatherer Called From Multiple Threads

## Observation

In most cases, a Stream Gatherer instantiated with `ofSequential()` is invoked from a single thread.

However, sometimes, the gatherer's integrator's `integrate()` method is called from multiple threads (though never concurrently).

## Question

Why would the stream pipeline incur the overhead of memory barriers for the state object to execute the `integrate()` method in different threads – when it could just as well execute it in a single thread without memory barriers?

## Proof (at Simone's Repeated Request ;-)

In this package, you'll find the `ParallelStreamWithSequentialGatherer` class.

It will
1. Create a list of 1000 `Integer` objects (0–999).
1. Create a parallel stream of the list. 
1. Map them in parallel to an `ElementStage1` (which stores the element, the thread which called the `map()` operation, and the thread-local sequence number). 
1. Map them with a sequential gatherer to an `ElementStage2` (which stores all the data of `ElementStage1` plus the thread which called the `integrate()` method, the thread-local sequence number, and a global sequence number). 
1. Map them in parallel to an `ElementStage3` (which stores all the data of `ElementStage2` plus the thread which called the `map()` operation and the thread-local sequence number). 
1. Print some statistics.

It will repeat this 10,000 times and print out statistics about how many threads called the sequential gatherer. 
You'll see these statistics in the next section.

### Statistics

Interestingly, the more objects go through the stream, the fewer threads are used by the sequential gatherer:

#### For 100 Integer objects:
```
# of threads
1 (main)   ->  471 x ( 4.71 %)
1 (worker) -> 2743 x (27.43 %)
2          -> 4424 x (44.24 %)
3          -> 1856 x (18.56 %)
4          ->  401 x ( 4.01 %)
5          ->   92 x ( 0.92 %)
6          ->   13 x ( 0.13 %)
```

#### For 1,000 Integer objects:
```
# of threads
1 (main)   ->  767 x ( 7.67 %)
1 (worker) -> 6261 x (62.61 %)
2          -> 2433 x (24.33 %)
3          ->  441 x ( 4.41 %)
4          ->   83 x ( 0.83 %)
5          ->   11 x ( 0.11 %)
6          ->    4 x ( 0.04 %)
```

#### For 10,000 Integer objects:
```
# of threads
1 (main)   ->  526 x ( 5.26 %)
1 (worker) -> 8714 x (87.14 %)
2          ->  706 x ( 7.06 %)
3          ->   50 x ( 0.50 %)
4          ->    3 x ( 0.03 %)
5          ->    1 x ( 0.01 %)
```

#### For 100,000 Integer objects:
```
# of threads
1 (main)   ->  552 x ( 5.52 %)
1 (worker) -> 9242 x (92.42 %)
2          ->  203 x ( 2.03 %)
3          ->    2 x ( 0.02 %)
4          ->    1 x ( 0.01 %)
```

#### For 1,000,000 Integer objects:
```
# of threads
1 (main)   ->  419 x ( 4.19 %)
1 (worker) -> 9472 x (94.72 %)
2          ->  108 x ( 1.08 %)
3          ->    1 x ( 0.01 %)
```

## Sample Output

Here's the output of a run where the `integrate()` method was called by two gatherer threads.

What you can see is:
* In the first stage, *worker-10* processes 75 elements in the following order:\
  202–217 (16), 194–201 (8), 187–193 (7) 15–30 (16), 7–14 (8), 0–6 (7), 937–943 (7), 531–537 (7)
* In the first stage, *worker-3* processes 8 elements: 77–84
* In the second stage, *worker-10* processes 77 elements: 0–76
* The remaining elements, 77–999 were all processed by *worker-3*.
* Interestingly, element 77 was also the first one processed by *worker-3* in the first stage.

